   <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #8CC7FF; color:black; font-size:100%; <p>\n       <b>\n        Greek\n       </b>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">\n        Modern Greek\n       </a>\n       :\n       <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">\n        \xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xce\xbd\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xce\xac\n       </span>\n       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">\n         [elini\xcb\x88ka]\n        </a>\n       </span>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        ellinik\xc3\xa1\n       </i>\n       , "Greek",\n       <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">\n        \xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xce\xbd\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xce\xae \xce\xb3\xce\xbb\xcf\x8e\xcf\x83\xcf\x83\xce\xb1\n       </span>\n       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">\n         [elini\xcb\x88ci \xcb\x88\xc9\xa3losa]\n        </a>\n       </span>\n       <small class="nowrap">\n        (\n        <span class="unicode haudio">\n         <span class="fn">\n          <span style="white-space:nowrap">\n           <a href="/wiki/File:Elliniki_Glossa.ogg" title="About this sound">\n            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>\n           </a>\n          </span>\n          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Elliniki_Glossa.ogg" title="Elliniki Glossa.ogg">\n           listen\n          </a>\n         </span>\n        </span>\n        )\n       </small>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        ellinik\xc3\xad gl\xc3\xb3ssa\n       </i>\n       , "Greek language") is an independent branch of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">\n        Indo-European\n       </a>\n       family of languages, native to\n       <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">\n        Greece\n       </a>\n       and other parts of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">\n        Eastern Mediterranean\n       </a>\n       . It has the longest documented history of any living Indo-European language, spanning 34 centuries of written records.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Its writing system has been the\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">\n        Greek alphabet\n       </a>\n       for the major part of its history; other systems, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">\n        Linear B\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">\n        Cypriot syllabary\n       </a>\n       , were used previously.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The alphabet arose from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">\n        Phoenician script\n       </a>\n       and was in turn the basis of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">\n        Latin\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic" title="Cyrillic">\n        Cyrillic\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Armenian_alphabet" title="Armenian alphabet">\n        Armenian\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">\n        Coptic\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">\n        Gothic\n       </a>\n       and many other writing systems.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Greek language holds an important place in the history of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">\n        Western world\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">\n        Christianity\n       </a>\n       ; the canon of\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">\n        ancient Greek literature\n       </a>\n       includes seminal works in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Western_literature" title="Western literature">\n        Western canon\n       </a>\n       such as the epic poems\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">\n         Iliad\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       and\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">\n         Odyssey\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       . Greek is also the language in which many of the foundational texts in science, especially astronomy, mathematics and logic, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">\n        Western philosophy\n       </a>\n       , such as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_dialogue" title="Platonic dialogue">\n        Platonic dialogues\n       </a>\n       and the works of\n       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">\n        Aristotle\n       </a>\n       , are composed; the\n       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">\n        New Testament\n       </a>\n       of the Christian\n       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">\n        Bible\n       </a>\n       was written in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_Greek" title="Koin\xc3\xa9 Greek">\n        Koin\xc3\xa9 Greek\n       </a>\n       . Together with the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">\n        Latin\n       </a>\n       texts and traditions of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_world" title="Roman world">\n        Roman world\n       </a>\n       , the study of the Greek texts and society of antiquity constitutes the discipline of\n       <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">\n        Classics\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       During\n       <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">\n        antiquity\n       </a>\n       , Greek was a widely spoken\n       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">\n        lingua franca\n       </a>\n       in the Mediterranean world and many places beyond. It would eventually become the official parlance of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">\n        Byzantine Empire\n       </a>\n       and develop into\n       <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">\n        Medieval Greek\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In its\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">\n        modern form\n       </a>\n       , the Greek language is the official language in two countries, Greece and Cyprus, a recognised minority language in seven other countries, and is one of the 24\n       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union#Official_EU_languages" title="Languages of the European Union">\n        official languages of the European Union\n       </a>\n       . The language is spoken by at least 13.2 million people today in Greece, Cyprus, Italy, Albania, Turkey, and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">\n        Greek diaspora\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Greek\n       <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">\n        roots\n       </a>\n       are often used to coin new words for other languages; Greek and\n       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">\n        Latin\n       </a>\n       are the predominant sources of\n       <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">\n        international scientific vocabulary\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homer_British_Museum.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="635" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/180px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/270px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/360px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="180"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homer_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Idealised portrayal of\n         <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">\n          Homer\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div  </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">\n        History\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greek" title="History of Greek">\n        History of Greek\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Greek has been spoken in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">\n        Balkan peninsula\n       </a>\n       since around the 3rd millennium BC,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BVG_6-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-BVG-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       or possibly earlier.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The earliest written evidence is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">\n        Linear B\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">\n        clay tablet\n       </a>\n       found in\n       <a href="/wiki/Messenia" title="Messenia">\n        Messenia\n       </a>\n       that dates to between 1450 and 1350 BC,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       making Greek the world\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">\n        oldest recorded\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_language" title="Modern language">\n        living language\n       </a>\n       . Among the Indo-European languages, its date of earliest written attestation is matched only by the now extinct\n       <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">\n        Anatolian languages\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Periods">\n        Periods\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Periods">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="460" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/220px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/330px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/440px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">\n          Proto-Greek\n         </a>\n         -speaking area according to linguist\n         <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_I._Georgiev" title="Vladimir I. Georgiev">\n          Vladimir I. Georgiev\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The Greek language is conventionally divided into the following periods:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">\n          Proto-Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        : the unrecorded but assumed last ancestor of all known varieties of Greek. The unity of Proto-Greek would have ended as Hellenic migrants entered the\n        <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Greece" title="Geography of Greece">\n         Greek peninsula\n        </a>\n        sometime in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">\n         Neolithic\n        </a>\n        era or the\n        <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">\n         Bronze Age\n        </a>\n        .\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n         <a href="#cite_note-9">\n          [9]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">\n          Mycenaean Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        : the language of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">\n         Mycenaean civilisation\n        </a>\n        . It is recorded in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">\n         Linear B\n        </a>\n        script on tablets dating from the 15th century BC onwards.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">\n          Ancient Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        : in its various\n        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_dialects" title="Ancient Greek dialects">\n         dialects\n        </a>\n        , the language of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">\n         Archaic\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">\n         Classical\n        </a>\n        periods of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">\n         ancient Greek civilisation\n        </a>\n        . It was widely known throughout the\n        <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">\n         Roman Empire\n        </a>\n        . Ancient Greek fell into disuse in western Europe in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">\n         Middle Ages\n        </a>\n        , but remained officially in use in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">\n         Byzantine\n        </a>\n        world and was reintroduced to the rest of Europe with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">\n         Fall of Constantinople\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">\n         Greek\n        </a>\n        migration to western Europe.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">\n          Koine Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        : The fusion of\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greek" title="Ionian Greek">\n         Ionian\n        </a>\n        with\n        <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">\n         Attic\n        </a>\n        , the dialect of\n        <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">\n         Athens\n        </a>\n        , began the process that resulted in the creation of the first common Greek dialect, which became a\n        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">\n         lingua franca\n        </a>\n        across the\n        <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">\n         Eastern Mediterranean\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">\n         Near East\n        </a>\n        . Koine Greek can be initially traced within the armies and conquered territories of\n        <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">\n         Alexander the Great\n        </a>\n        and after the Hellenistic colonisation of the known world, it was spoken from\n        <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">\n         Egypt\n        </a>\n        to the fringes of\n        <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">\n         India\n        </a>\n        . After the\n        <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">\n         Roman\n        </a>\n        conquest of Greece, an unofficial\n        <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">\n         bilingualism\n        </a>\n        of Greek and\n        <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">\n         Latin\n        </a>\n        was established in the city of\n        <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">\n         Rome\n        </a>\n        and Koine Greek became a first or second language in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">\n         Roman Empire\n        </a>\n        . The origin of\n        <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">\n         Christianity\n        </a>\n        can also be traced through Koine Greek, because the\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">\n         Apostles\n        </a>\n        used this form of the language to spread Christianity. It is also known as\n        <b>\n         Hellenistic Greek\n        </b>\n        ,\n        <b>\n         New Testament Greek\n        </b>\n        , and sometimes\n        <b>\n         Biblical Greek\n        </b>\n        because it was the original language of the\n        <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">\n         New Testament\n        </a>\n        and the\n        <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">\n         Old Testament\n        </a>\n        was translated into the same language via the\n        <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">\n         Septuagint\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="399" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png/220px-Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png/330px-Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Distribution of varieties of Greek in\n         <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">\n          Anatolia\n         </a>\n         , 1910.\n         <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek#Demotic" title="Modern Greek">\n          Demotic\n         </a>\n         in yellow.\n         <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">\n          Pontic\n         </a>\n         in orange.\n         <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">\n          Cappadocian Greek\n         </a>\n         in green, with green dots indicating individual Cappadocian Greek villages.\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n          <a href="#cite_note-10">\n           [10]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">\n          Medieval Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        , also known as\n        <b>\n         Byzantine Greek\n        </b>\n        : the continuation of Koine Greek, up to the demise of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">\n         Byzantine Empire\n        </a>\n        in the 15th century.\n        <i>\n         Medieval Greek\n        </i>\n        is a cover phrase for a whole continuum of different speech and writing styles, ranging from vernacular continuations of spoken Koine that were already approaching\n        <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">\n         Modern Greek\n        </a>\n        in many respects, to highly learned forms imitating classical Attic. Much of the written Greek that was used as the official language of the Byzantine Empire was an eclectic middle-ground variety based on the tradition of written Koine.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">\n          Modern Greek\n         </a>\n        </b>\n        (Neo-Hellenic):\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_11-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-11">\n          [11]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        Stemming from Medieval Greek, Modern Greek usages can be traced in the Byzantine period, as early as the 11th century. It is the language used by the modern Greeks, and, apart from Standard Modern Greek, there are several\n        <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Modern_Greek" title="Varieties of Modern Greek">\n         dialects\n        </a>\n        of it.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diglossia">\n        Diglossia\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diglossia">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language_question" title="Greek language question">\n        Greek language question\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In the modern era, the Greek language entered a state of\n       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">\n        diglossia\n       </a>\n       : the coexistence of vernacular and archaizing written forms of the language. What came to be known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language_question" title="Greek language question">\n        Greek language question\n       </a>\n       was a polarization between two competing varieties of\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">\n        Modern Greek\n       </a>\n       :\n       <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">\n        Dimotiki\n       </a>\n       , the vernacular form of Modern Greek proper, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">\n        Katharevousa\n       </a>\n       , meaning \'purified\', a compromise between Dimotiki and\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">\n        Ancient Greek\n       </a>\n       , which was developed in the early 19th century and was used for literary and official purposes in the newly formed Greek state. In 1976, Dimotiki was declared the official language of Greece, having incorporated features of Katharevousa and giving birth to\n       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Modern_Greek#Standard_Modern_Greek" title="Varieties of Modern Greek">\n        Standard Modern Greek\n       </a>\n       , which is used today for all official purposes and in education.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_unity">\n        Historical unity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Historical unity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="750" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/240px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/360px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/480px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png 2x" width="240"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The distribution of major modern Greek dialect areas.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The historical unity and continuing identity between the various stages of the Greek language is often emphasised. Although Greek has undergone morphological and phonological changes comparable to those seen in other languages, never since classical antiquity has its cultural, literary, and orthographic tradition been interrupted to the extent that one can speak of a new language emerging. Greek speakers today still tend to regard literary works of ancient Greek as part of their own rather than a foreign language.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is also often stated that the historical changes have been relatively slight compared with some other languages. According to one estimation, "Homeric Greek is probably closer to demotic than 12-century Middle English is to modern spoken English."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">\n        Geographic distribution\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">\n        Greeks\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">\n        Greek diaspora\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A27_Motorway,_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_(A2)_-_02.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2601" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg/220px-A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg/330px-A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg/440px-A27_Motorway%2C_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_%28A2%29_-_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A27_Motorway,_Greece_-_Section_Kozani-Ptolemaida_-_Kozani-North_interchange_(A2)_-_02.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Greek language road sign, A27 Motorway, Greece\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="959" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg/240px-Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg/360px-Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg/480px-Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg.png 2x" width="240"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_USC2000_PHS.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Spread of Greek in the United States.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Greek is spoken by about 13 million people, mainly in\n       <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">\n        Greece\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">\n        Albania\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">\n        Cyprus\n       </a>\n       , but also worldwide by the large\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">\n        Greek diaspora\n       </a>\n       . There are traditional Greek-speaking settlements and regions in the neighbouring countries of\n       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">\n        Albania\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">\n        Bulgaria\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">\n        Turkey\n       </a>\n       , as well as in several countries in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">\n        Black Sea\n       </a>\n       area, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">\n        Ukraine\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">\n        Russia\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">\n        Romania\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">\n        Georgia\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">\n        Armenia\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">\n        Azerbaijan\n       </a>\n       , and around the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">\n        Mediterranean Sea\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">\n        Southern Italy\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">\n        Syria\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">\n        Israel\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">\n        Egypt\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">\n        Lebanon\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">\n        Libya\n       </a>\n       and ancient coastal towns along the\n       <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">\n        Levant\n       </a>\n       . The language is also spoken by Greek emigrant communities in many countries in\n       <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">\n        Western Europe\n       </a>\n       , especially the United Kingdom and Germany, Canada, the United States,\n       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">\n        Australia\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">\n        Argentina\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">\n        Brazil\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">\n        Chile\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">\n        South Africa\n       </a>\n       and others.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">\n        Official status\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Official status">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Greek is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">\n        official language\n       </a>\n       of Greece, where it is spoken by almost the entire population.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is also the official language of Cyprus (nominally alongside\n       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">\n        Turkish\n       </a>\n       ).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekCyprus_16-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GreekCyprus-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Because of the membership of Greece and Cyprus in the European Union, Greek is one of the organization\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union#Official_EU_languages" title="Languages of the European Union">\n        24 official languages\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_Union_17-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-European_Union-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Furthermore, Greek is officially recognised as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">\n        minority language\n       </a>\n       in parts of\n       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">\n        Italy\n       </a>\n       and official in\n       <a href="/wiki/Dropull" title="Dropull">\n        Dropull\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Himara" title="Himara">\n        Himara\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">\n        Albania\n       </a>\n       ) and as a minority language all over\n       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">\n        Albania\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HumanRights_18-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-HumanRights-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       as well as in\n       <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">\n        Lebanon\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">\n        Syria\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">\n        Armenia\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">\n        Romania\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">\n        Ukraine\n       </a>\n       as a regional or minority language in the framework of the\n       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">\n        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CouncilofEurope_19-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CouncilofEurope-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Greeks are also a\n       <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Hungary" title="Greeks in Hungary">\n        recognised ethnic minority\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">\n        Hungary\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">\n        Characteristics\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Characteristics">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_grammar" title="Ancient Greek grammar">\n        Ancient Greek grammar\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek_grammar" title="Koine Greek grammar">\n        Koine Greek grammar\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_grammar" title="Modern Greek grammar">\n        Modern Greek grammar\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">\n        phonology\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">\n        morphology\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">\n        syntax\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">\n        vocabulary\n       </a>\n       of the language show both conservative and innovative tendencies across the entire attestation of the language from the ancient to the modern period. The division into conventional periods is, as with all such periodisations, relatively arbitrary, especially because at all periods, Ancient Greek has enjoyed high prestige, and the literate borrowed heavily from it.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">\n        Phonology\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phonology">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_phonology" title="Modern Greek phonology">\n        Modern Greek phonology\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Across its history, the syllabic structure of Greek has varied little: Greek shows a mixed syllable structure, permitting complex syllabic onsets but very restricted codas. It has only oral vowels and a fairly stable set of consonantal contrasts. The main phonological changes occurred during the Hellenistic and Roman period (see\n       <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek_phonology" title="Koine Greek phonology">\n        Koine Greek phonology\n       </a>\n       for details):\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        replacement of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">\n         pitch accent\n        </a>\n        with a\n        <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">\n         stress accent\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        simplification of the system of\n        <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">\n         vowels\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">\n         diphthongs\n        </a>\n        : loss of vowel length distinction, monophthongisation of most diphthongs and several steps in a\n        <a href="/wiki/Chain_shift" title="Chain shift">\n         chain shift\n        </a>\n        of vowels towards /i/ (\n        <a href="/wiki/Iotacism" title="Iotacism">\n         iotacism\n        </a>\n        ).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        development of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">\n         voiceless\n        </a>\n        <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">\n         aspirated\n        </a>\n        <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">\n         plosives\n        </a>\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /p\xca\xb0/\n        </span>\n        and\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /t\xca\xb0/\n        </span>\n        to the voiceless\n        <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">\n         fricatives\n        </a>\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /f/\n        </span>\n        and\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /\xce\xb8/\n        </span>\n        , respectively; the similar development of\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /k\xca\xb0/\n        </span>\n        to\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /x/\n        </span>\n        may have taken place later (the phonological changes are not reflected in the orthography, and both earlier and later phonemes are written with\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phi_(letter)" title="Phi (letter)">\n         \xcf\x86\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Theta" title="Theta">\n         \xce\xb8\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a href="/wiki/Chi_(letter)" title="Chi (letter)">\n         \xcf\x87\n        </a>\n        ).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        development of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">\n         voiced\n        </a>\n        plosives\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /b/\n        </span>\n        ,\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /d/\n        </span>\n        , and\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /\xc9\xa1/\n        </span>\n        to their voiced fricative counterparts\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /\xce\xb2/\n        </span>\n        (later /v/),\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /\xc3\xb0/\n        </span>\n        , and\n        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">\n         /\xc9\xa3/\n        </span>\n        .\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">\n        Morphology\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Morphology">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       In all its stages, the morphology of Greek shows an extensive set of productive derivational affixes, a limited but productive system of compounding\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and a rich inflectional system. Although its morphological categories have been fairly stable over time, morphological changes are present throughout, particularly in the nominal and verbal systems. The major change in the nominal morphology since the classical stage was the disuse of the dative case (its functions being largely taken over by the genitive). The verbal system has lost the infinitive, the synthetically-formed future and perfect tenses and the optative mood. Many have been replaced by\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periphrastic" title="Periphrastic">\n        periphrastic\n       </a>\n       (analytical) forms.\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns_and_adjectives">\n        Nouns and adjectives\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nouns and adjectives">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       Pronouns show distinctions in\n       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">\n        person\n       </a>\n       (1st, 2nd, and 3rd),\n       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">\n        number\n       </a>\n       (singular, dual, and plural in the ancient language; singular and plural alone in later stages), and\n       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">\n        gender\n       </a>\n       (masculine, feminine, and neuter) and decline for\n       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">\n        case\n       </a>\n       (from six cases in the earliest forms attested to four in the modern language).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Nouns, articles and adjectives show all the distinctions except for person. Both attributive and predicative adjectives agree with the noun.\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">\n        Verbs\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Verbs">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       The inflectional categories of the Greek verb have likewise remained largely the same over the course of the language\'s history but with significant changes in the number of distinctions within each category and their morphological expression. Greek verbs have\n       <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">\n        synthetic\n       </a>\n       inflectional forms for:\n      </p>\n      <table class="wikitable">\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Ancient Greek\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Modern Greek\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         Person\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         first, second and third\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         also\n         <a href="/wiki/T%E2%80%93V_distinction" title="T\xe2\x80\x93V distinction">\n          second person formal\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         Number\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         singular,\n         <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">\n          dual\n         </a>\n         and plural\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         singular and plural\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">\n          tense\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">\n          present\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">\n          past\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">\n          future\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         past and non-past (future is expressed by a periphrastic construction)\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">\n          aspect\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Imperfective_aspect" title="Imperfective aspect">\n          imperfective\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Perfective_aspect" title="Perfective aspect">\n          perfective\n         </a>\n         (traditionally called\n         <i>\n          <a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">\n           aorist\n          </a>\n         </i>\n         ) and\n         <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">\n          perfect\n         </a>\n         (sometimes also called\n         <i>\n          perfective\n         </i>\n         ; see\n         <a href="/wiki/Perfective_aspect#Perfective_vs._perfect" title="Perfective aspect">\n          note about terminology\n         </a>\n         )\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         imperfective and perfective/aorist (perfect is expressed by a periphrastic construction)\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">\n          mood\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Realis_mood" title="Realis mood">\n          indicative\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">\n          subjunctive\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">\n          imperative\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Optative_mood" title="Optative mood">\n          optative\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         indicative, subjunctive,\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">\n          <a href="#cite_note-22">\n           [22]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n         and imperative (other modal functions are expressed by periphrastic constructions)\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         Voice\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Active_voice" title="Active voice">\n          active\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_voice" title="Middle voice">\n          middle\n         </a>\n         , and\n         <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">\n          passive\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         active and\n         <a href="/wiki/Mediopassive_voice" title="Mediopassive voice">\n          medio-passive\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">\n        Syntax\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Syntax">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Many aspects of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">\n        syntax\n       </a>\n       of Greek have remained constant: verbs agree with their subject only, the use of the surviving cases is largely intact (nominative for subjects and predicates, accusative for objects of most verbs and many prepositions, genitive for possessors), articles precede nouns, adpositions are largely prepositional, relative clauses follow the noun they modify and relative pronouns are clause-initial. However, the morphological changes also have their counterparts in the syntax, and there are also significant differences between the syntax of the ancient and that of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_grammar" title="Modern Greek grammar">\n        modern form of the language\n       </a>\n       . Ancient Greek made great use of participial constructions and of constructions involving the infinitive, and the modern variety lacks the infinitive entirely (instead having a raft of new periphrastic constructions) and uses participles more restrictively. The loss of the dative led to a rise of prepositional indirect objects (and the use of the genitive to directly mark these as well). Ancient Greek tended to be verb-final, but neutral word order in the modern language is VSO or SVO.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">\n        Vocabulary\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Greek is a language distinguished by an extensive\n       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">\n        vocabulary\n       </a>\n       . Most of the vocabulary of Ancient Greek was inherited, but it includes a number of\n       <a href="/wiki/Pre-Greek_substrate" title="Pre-Greek substrate">\n        borrowings\n       </a>\n       from the languages of the populations that inhabited Greece before the arrival of Proto-Greeks.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Words of non-Indo-European origin can be traced into Greek from as early as Mycenaean times; they include a large number of Greek\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponym" title="Toponym">\n        toponyms\n       </a>\n       . The vast majority of Modern Greek vocabulary is directly inherited from Ancient Greek, but in some cases, words have changed meanings.\n       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">\n        Loanwords\n       </a>\n       (words of foreign origin) have entered the language mainly from\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">\n        Latin\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language#History" title="Venetian language">\n        Venetian\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">\n        Turkish\n       </a>\n       . During the older periods of Greek, loanwords into Greek acquired Greek inflections, thus leaving only a foreign root word. Modern borrowings (from the 20th century on), especially from\n       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">\n        French\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">\n        English\n       </a>\n       , are typically not inflected.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_loanwords_in_other_languages">\n        Greek loanwords in other languages\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Greek loanwords in other languages">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       For more details on this topic, see\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_and_Latin_roots_in_English" title="Greek and Latin roots in English">\n        Greek and Latin roots in English\n       </a>\n       .\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Greek words have been widely borrowed into other languages, including English:\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">\n         mathematics\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">\n         physics\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">\n         astronomy\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">\n         democracy\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">\n         philosophy\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/athletics" title="wikt:athletics">\n         athletics\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">\n         theatre\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">\n         rhetoric\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">\n         baptism\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/evangelist" title="wikt:evangelist">\n         evangelist\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , etc. Moreover, Greek words and\n       <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">\n        word elements\n       </a>\n       continue to be productive as a basis for coinages:\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">\n         anthropology\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">\n         photography\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Telephony" title="Telephony">\n         telephony\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">\n         isomer\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">\n         biomechanics\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">\n         cinematography\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , etc. and form, with\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Latin" title="Scientific Latin">\n        Latin words\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">\n        foundation of international scientific and technical vocabulary\n       </a>\n       like all words ending with\n       <i>\n        \xe2\x80\x93logy\n       </i>\n       ("discourse"). There are many\n       <a href="/wiki/English_words_of_Greek_origin" title="English words of Greek origin">\n        English words of Greek origin\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">\n        Classification\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Classification">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Greek is an independent branch of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">\n        Indo-European\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">\n        language family\n       </a>\n       . The ancient language most closely related to it may be\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_language" title="Ancient Macedonian language">\n        ancient Macedonian\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which many scholars suggest may have been a\n       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">\n        dialect\n       </a>\n       of Greek itself, but it is so poorly attested that it is difficult to conclude anything about it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Independently of the Macedonian question, some scholars have grouped Greek into\n       <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Phrygian" title="Graeco-Phrygian">\n        Graeco-Phrygian\n       </a>\n       , as Greek and the extinct\n       <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_language" title="Phrygian language">\n        Phrygian\n       </a>\n       share features that are not found in other Indo-European languages.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Among living languages, some Indo-Europeanists suggest that Greek may be most closely related to\n       <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">\n        Armenian\n       </a>\n       (see\n       <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Armenian" title="Graeco-Armenian">\n        Graeco-Armenian\n       </a>\n       ) or the\n       <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">\n        Indo-Iranian languages\n       </a>\n       (see\n       <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Aryan" title="Graeco-Aryan">\n        Graeco-Aryan\n       </a>\n       ), but little definitive evidence has been found for grouping the living branches of the family.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In addition,\n       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">\n        Albanian\n       </a>\n       has also been considered somewhat related to Greek and\n       <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">\n        Armenian\n       </a>\n       by some linguists. If proven and recognised, the three languages would form a new Balkan sub-branch with other dead European languages.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">\n        Writing system\n       </span>\n       <span   v\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-talk">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Greek_alphabet_sidebar" title="Template talk:Greek alphabet href="/wiki/Greek_Braille" title="Greek Braille">\n        Greek Braille\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_B">\n        Linear B\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Linear B">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">\n        Linear B\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">\n        Linear B\n       </a>\n       , attested as early as the late 15th century BC, was the first script used to write Greek.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:0-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is basically a\n       <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">\n        syllabary\n       </a>\n       , which was finally deciphered by\n       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ventris" title="Michael Ventris">\n        Michael Ventris\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Chadwick" title="John Chadwick">\n        John Chadwick\n       </a>\n       in the 1950s (its precursor,\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">\n        Linear A\n       </a>\n       , has not been deciphered to this day).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_30-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:0-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The language of the Linear B texts,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek_language" title="Mycenaean Greek language">\n        Mycenaean Greek\n       </a>\n       , is the earliest known form of Greek.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_30-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-:0-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cypriot_syllabary">\n        Cypriot syllabary\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cypriot syllabary">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">\n        Cypriot syllabary\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="422" height="417" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg/220px-Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg/330px-Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Greek inscription in Cypriot syllabic script\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Another similar system used to write the Greek language was the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">\n        Cypriot syllabary\n       </a>\n       (also a descendant of\n       <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">\n        Linear A\n       </a>\n       via the intermediate\n       <a href="/wiki/Cypro-Minoan_syllabary" title="Cypro-Minoan syllabary">\n        Cypro-Minoan syllabary\n       </a>\n       ), which is closely related to Linear B but uses somewhat different syllabic conventions to represent phoneme sequences. The Cypriot syllabary is attested in\n       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">\n        Cyprus\n       </a>\n       from the 11th century BC until its gradual abandonment in the late Classical period, in favor of the standard Greek alphabet.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_alphabet">\n        Greek alphabet\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Greek alphabet">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main articles:\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">\n        Greek alphabet\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Greek_orthography" title="Greek orthography">\n        Greek orthography\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_alphabet_variants.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="495" height="427" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/220px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/330px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/440px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_alphabet_variants.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Ancient epichoric variants of the Greek alphabet from\n         <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">\n          Euboea\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">\n          Ionia\n         </a>\n         ,\n         <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">\n          Athens\n         </a>\n         , and\n         <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">\n          Corinth\n         </a>\n         comparing to modern Greek.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Greek has been written in the Greek alphabet since approximately the 9th century BC. It was created by modifying the\n       <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">\n        Phoenician alphabet\n       </a>\n       , with the innovation of adopting certain letters to represent the\n       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">\n        vowels\n       </a>\n       . The variant of the alphabet in use today is essentially the late\n       <a href="/wiki/Ionic_Greek" title="Ionic Greek">\n        Ionic\n       </a>\n       variant, introduced for writing classical\n       <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">\n        Attic\n       </a>\n       in 403 BC. In classical Greek, as in classical Latin, only upper-case letters existed. The lower-case Greek letters were developed much later by medieval scribes to permit a faster, more convenient cursive writing style with the use of\n       <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">\n        ink\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Quill" title="Quill">\n        quill\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The Greek alphabet consists of 24 letters, each with an uppercase (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_letter" title="Capital letter">\n        majuscule\n       </a>\n       ) and lowercase (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_case" title="Lower case">\n        minuscule\n       </a>\n       ) form. The letter\n       <a href="/wiki/Sigma" title="Sigma">\n        sigma\n       </a>\n       has an additional lowercase form (\xcf\x82) used in the final position:\n      </p>\n      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">\n       <tr>\n        <th colspan="33">\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_case" title="Upper case">\n          upper case\n         </a>\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Alpha" title="Alpha">\n          \xce\x91\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         <a  <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Delta_(letter)" title="Delta (letter)">\n          \xce\x94\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Epsilon" title="Epsilon">\n          \xce\x95\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Zeta" title="Zeta">\n          \xce\x96\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Eta"
